<td>Photovoltaic power systems and components:<br/>
<p><b>Top:</b> solar string <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">inverter</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_system" title="Balance of system">BOS</a> components <b>·</b> <a href="#Solar_array">Solar array</a> on rooftop in Hong Kong, China <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaics" title="Building-integrated photovoltaics">BIPV</a> on balcony in Helsinki, Finland<br/>
<b>Middle:</b> <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">rooftop system</a> in Boston, United States <b>·</b>  Westmill <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">solar park</a> in the United Kingdom <b>·</b>  Dual axis <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">tracker</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrating_photovoltaics" title="Concentrating photovoltaics">CPV</a> modules <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaic_power_stations#World.27s_largest_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of photovoltaic power stations">world’s largest</a> solar power station, as seen from space<br/>
<b>Bottom:</b> commercial rooftop PV system of about 400 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watt-peak" title="Watt-peak">kW<sub>p</sub></a> <b>·</b> <a href="/wiki/Komekurayama_Solar_Power_Plant" title="Komekurayama Solar Power Plant">Power plant</a> on Mt. Komekura, Japan <b>·</b>  Solar PV system on Germany's highest mountain-top</p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>A <b>photovoltaic system</b>, also <b>PV system</b> or <b>solar power system</b>, is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_system" title="Power system">power system</a> designed to supply usable <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaics" title="Photovoltaics">photovoltaics</a>. It consists of an arrangement of several components, including <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a> to absorb and convert sunlight into electricity, a <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">solar inverter</a> to change the electric current from DC to AC, as well as <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_mounting_system" title="Photovoltaic mounting system">mounting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cable" title="Solar cable">cabling</a> and other electrical accessories to set up a working system. It may also use a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_tracking_system" title="Solar tracking system">solar tracking system</a> to improve the system's overall performance and include an <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">integrated battery solution</a>, as prices for storage devices are expected to decline. Strictly speaking, a <b>solar array</b> only encompasses the ensemble of solar panels, the visible part of the PV system, and does not include all the other hardware, often summarized as <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_system" title="Balance of system">balance of system</a> (BOS). Moreover, PV systems convert light directly into electricity and shouldn't be confused with other technologies, such as <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> or <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_energy" title="Solar thermal energy">solar thermal</a>, used for heating and cooling.</p>
<p>PV systems range from small, <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">rooftop-mounted</a> or <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaics" title="Building-integrated photovoltaics">building-integrated</a> systems with capacities from a few to several tens of kilowatts, to large <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">utility-scale power stations</a> of hundreds of megawatts. Nowadays, most PV systems are <a href="/wiki/Grid-connected_photovoltaic_power_system" title="Grid-connected photovoltaic power system">grid-connected</a>, while off-grid or <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">stand-alone systems</a> only account for a small portion of the market.</p>
<p>Operating silently and without any moving parts or <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">environmental emissions</a>, PV systems have developed from being niche market applications into a mature technology used for mainstream electricity generation. A rooftop system <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon#Energy_payback_time" title="Crystalline silicon">recoups</a> the invested energy for its manufacturing and installation within 0.7 to 2 years and produces about 95 percent of net clean <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> over a 30-year service lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economics-of-PV-Liebreich_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economics-of-PV-Liebreich-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to the exponential <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">growth of photovoltaics</a>, prices for PV systems have rapidly declined in recent years. However, they vary by market and the size of the system. In 2014, prices for residential 5-kilowatt <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">systems in the United States</a> were around $3.29 per watt,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> while in the highly penetrated <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">German market</a>, prices for rooftop systems of up to 100 kW declined to €1.24 per watt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Nowadays, solar PV modules account for less than half of the system's overall cost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> leaving the rest to the remaining BOS-components and to soft costs, which include customer acquisition, permitting, inspection and interconnection, installation labor and financing costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Modern_system"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Modern system</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_system">Modern system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Modern system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2406" data-file-width="2927" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg/220px-From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg/330px-From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg/440px-From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:From_a_solar_cell_to_a_PV_system.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of the possible components of a photovoltaic system</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" title="Photovoltaic">photovoltaic</a> system converts the sun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">radiation</a> into usable <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. It comprises the solar array and the balance of system components. PV systems can be categorized by various aspects, such as, <a href="/wiki/Grid-connected_photovoltaic_power_system" title="Grid-connected photovoltaic power system">grid-connected</a> vs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stand_alone_power_system" title="Stand alone power system">stand alone</a> systems, building-integrated vs. rack-mounted systems, residential vs. utility systems, <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">distributed</a> vs. centralized systems, rooftop vs. ground-mounted systems, tracking vs. fixed-tilt systems, and new constructed vs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrofitted" title="Retrofitted">retrofitted</a> systems. Other distinctions may include, systems with microinverters vs. central inverter, systems using <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Thin-film_solar_cell" title="Thin-film solar cell">thin-film technology</a>, and systems with modules from Chinese vs. European and U.S.-manufacturers.</p>
<p>About 99 percent of all European and 90 percent of all U.S. solar power systems are connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>, while off-grid systems are somewhat more common in Australia and South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-outlook-2014_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-epia-outlook-2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup> PV systems rarely use battery storage. This may change soon, as government incentives for distributed energy storage are being implemented and investments in storage solutions are gradually becoming economically viable for small systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethz-harvard_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ethz-harvard-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> A solar array of a typical residential PV system is rack-mounted on the roof, rather than integrated into the roof or facade of the building, as this is significantly more expensive. Utility-scale <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">solar power stations</a> are ground-mounted, with fixed tilted solar panels rather than using expensive tracking devices. Crystalline silicon is the predominant material used in 90 percent of worldwide produced solar modules, while rival thin-film has lost market-share in recent years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17–20</sup> About 70 percent of all solar cells and modules are produced in China and Taiwan, leaving only 5 percent to European and US-<a href="/wiki/List_of_photovoltaics_companies" title="List of photovoltaics companies">manufacturers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:11–12</sup> The installed capacity for both, small rooftop systems and large solar power stations is growing rapidly and in equal parts, although there is a notable trend towards utility-scale systems, as the focus on new installations is shifting away from Europe to sunnier regions, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunbelt" title="Sunbelt">Sunbelt</a> in the U.S., which are less opposed to ground-mounted solar farms and cost-effectiveness is more emphasized by investors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-outlook-2014_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-epia-outlook-2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43</sup></p>
<p>Driven by advances in technology and increases in manufacturing scale and sophistication, the cost of photovoltaics is declining continuously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economics-of-PV-Liebreich_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Economics-of-PV-Liebreich-3">[3]</a></sup> There are several million PV systems distributed all over the world, mostly in Europe, with 1.4 million systems in Germany alone<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:5</sup>– as well as North America with 440,000 systems in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The energy <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell_efficiency" title="Solar cell efficiency">conversion efficiency</a> of a conventional solar module increased from 15 to 20 percent over the last 10 years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:17</sup> and a PV system recoups the energy needed for its manufacture in about 2 years. In exceptionally irradiated locations, or when thin-film technology is used, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon#Energy_payback_time" title="Crystalline silicon">energy payback time</a> decreases to one year or less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:30–33</sup> <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">Net metering</a> and financial incentives, such as preferential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariffs" title="Feed-in tariffs">feed-in tariffs</a> for solar-generated electricity, have also greatly supported installations of PV systems in many countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Levelised_cost_of_electricity" title="Levelised cost of electricity">levelised cost of electricity</a> from large-scale PV systems has become competitive with conventional electricity sources in an expanding list of geographic regions, and <a href="/wiki/Grid_parity" title="Grid parity">grid parity</a> has been achieved in about 30 different countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Branker_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Branker-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deutsche-2015-chasm_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-deutsche-2015-chasm-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics" title="Growth of photovoltaics">fast-growing global PV market</a> is rapidly approaching the 200 GW mark – about 40 times the installed capacity of 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Photovoltaic systems currently contribute about 1 percent to worldwide electricity generation. <a href="/wiki/Growth_of_photovoltaics#Top_10_ranking_of_worldwide_photovoltaic_installation" title="Growth of photovoltaics">Top installers of PV systems</a> in terms of capacity are currently China, Japan and the United States, while half of the world's capacity is installed in Europe, with Germany and Italy supplying 7% to 8% of their respective domestic electricity consumption with solar PV.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-iea-pvps-snapshot-1992-2014-17">[17]</a></sup> The International Energy Agency expects <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a> to become the world's largest source of electricity by 2050, with solar photovoltaics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_thermal" title="Concentrated solar thermal">concentrated solar thermal</a> contributing 16% and 11% to the global demand, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grid-connection">Grid-connection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Grid-connection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grid-connected_photovoltaic_power_system" title="Grid-connected photovoltaic power system">Grid-connected photovoltaic power system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="975" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/240px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/360px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png/480px-PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PV-system-schematics-residential-Eng.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schematics of a typical residential PV system</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A grid connected system is connected to a larger independent grid (typically the public electricity grid) and feeds energy directly into the grid. This energy may be shared by a residential or commercial building before or after the revenue measurement point. The difference being whether the credited energy production is calculated independently of the customer's energy consumption (<a href="/wiki/Feed-in_tariff" title="Feed-in tariff">feed-in tariff</a>) or only on the difference of energy (<a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a>). Grid connected systems vary in size from residential (2–10 kW<sub>p</sub>) to solar power stations (up to 10s of MW<sub>p</sub>). This is a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decentralized_electricity_generation" title="Decentralized electricity generation">decentralized electricity generation</a>. The feeding of electricity into the grid requires the transformation of DC into AC by a special, synchronising <a href="/wiki/Grid-tie_inverter" title="Grid-tie inverter">grid-tie inverter</a>. In kilowatt-sized installations the DC side system voltage is as high as permitted (typically 1000V except US residential 600 V) to limit ohmic losses. Most modules (60 or 72 crystalline silicon cells) generate 160 W to 300 W at 36 volts. It is sometimes necessary or desirable to connect the modules partially in parallel rather than all in series. One set of modules connected in series is known as a 'string'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scale_of_system">Scale of system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scale of system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Photovoltaic systems are generally categorized into three distinct market segments: residential rooftop, commercial rooftop, and ground-mount utility-scale systems. Their capacities range from a few kilowatts to hundreds of megawatts. A typical residential system is around 10 kilowatts and mounted on a sloped roof, while commercial systems may reach a megawatt-scale and are generally installed on low-slope or even flat roofs. Although rooftop mounted systems are small and display a higher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cost_per_watt" title="Cost per watt">cost per watt</a> than large utility-scale installations, they account for the largest share in the market. There is, however, a growing trend towards bigger utility-scale power plants, especially in the "sunbelt" region of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-outlook-2014_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-epia-outlook-2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Utility-scale</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">Photovoltaic power station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Perovosolarstation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="1000" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Perovosolarstation.jpg/220px-Perovosolarstation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Perovosolarstation.jpg/330px-Perovosolarstation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Perovosolarstation.jpg/440px-Perovosolarstation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Perovosolarstation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Perovo_Solar_Park" title="Perovo Solar Park">Perovo Solar Park</a> in Ukraine</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>Large utility-scale <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_power_station" title="Photovoltaic power station">solar parks or farms</a> are <a href="/wiki/Power_station" title="Power station">power stations</a> and capable of providing an energy supply to large numbers of consumers. <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">Generated electricity</a> is fed into the transmission grid powered by central generation plants (grid-connected or grid-tied plant), or combined with one, or many, domestic electricity generators to feed into a small <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a> (hybrid plant). In rare cases generated electricity is stored or used directly by island/standalone plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> PV systems are generally designed in order to ensure the highest energy yield for a given investment. Some large photovoltaic power stations such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_Star" title="Solar Star">Solar Star</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waldpolenz_Solar_Park" title="Waldpolenz Solar Park">Waldpolenz Solar Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Topaz_Solar_Farm" title="Topaz Solar Farm">Topaz Solar Farm</a> cover tens or hundreds of hectares and have power outputs up to hundreds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Rooftop, mobile, and portable</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">Rooftop photovoltaic power station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/220px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/330px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg/440px-Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_panels_on_house_roof.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rooftop_photovoltaic_power_station" title="Rooftop photovoltaic power station">Rooftop system</a> near <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, USA.</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>A small PV system is capable of providing enough AC electricity to power a single home, or even an isolated device in the form of AC or DC electric. For example, military and civilian Earth observation <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellites</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Street_lights" title="Street lights">street lights</a>, construction and traffic signs, <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a>, solar-powered tents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Electric_aircraft" title="Electric aircraft">electric aircraft</a> may contain integrated photovoltaic systems to provide a primary or <a href="/wiki/Emergency_power_system" title="Emergency power system">auxiliary power</a> source in the form of AC or DC power, depending on the design and power demands. In 2013, rooftop systems accounted for 60 percent of worldwide installations. However, there is a trend away from rooftop and towards utility-scale PV systems, as the focus of new PV installations is also shifting from Europe to countries in the sunbelt region of the planet where opposition to ground-mounted solar farms is less accentuated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-outlook-2014_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-epia-outlook-2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:43</sup></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Portable and mobile PV systems provide electrical power independent of utility connections, for "off the grid" operation. Such systems are so commonly used on <a href="/wiki/Recreational_vehicle" title="Recreational vehicle">recreational vehicles</a> and boats that there are retailers specializing in these applications<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and products specifically targeted to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Since recreational vehicles (RV) normally carry batteries and operate lighting and other systems on nominally 12-volt DC power, RV PV systems normally operate in a voltage range chosen to charge 12-volt batteries directly, and addition of a PV system requires only panels, a charge controller, and wiring.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dt>Building-integrated</dt>
</dl>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Building-integrated_photovoltaics" title="Building-integrated photovoltaics">Building-integrated photovoltaics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG/220px-Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG/330px-Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG/440px-Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fa%C3%A7ana_Fotvoltaica_MNACTEC.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Building-applied_photovoltaics" title="Building-applied photovoltaics">BAPV</a> wall near Barcelona, Spain</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd>In urban and suburban areas, photovoltaic arrays are commonly used on rooftops to supplement power use; often the building will have a connection to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, in which case the energy produced by the PV array can be sold back to the <a href="/wiki/Utility" title="Utility">utility</a> in some sort of <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a> agreement. Some utilities, such as Solvay Electric in Solvay, NY, use the rooftops of commercial customers and telephone poles to support their use of PV panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solvay_Electric_Using_Solar_Panels_on_Telephone_Poles_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solvay_Electric_Using_Solar_Panels_on_Telephone_Poles-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Solar_tree" title="Solar tree">Solar trees</a> are arrays that, as the name implies, mimic the look of trees, provide shade, and at night can function as <a href="/wiki/Street_light" title="Street light">street lights</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance">Performance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Performance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Uncertainties in revenue over time relate mostly to the evaluation of the solar resource and to the performance of the system itself. In the best of cases, uncertainties are typically 4% for year-to-year climate variability, 5% for solar resource estimation (in a horizontal plane), 3% for estimation of irradiation in the plane of the array, 3% for power rating of modules, 2% for losses due to dirt and soiling, 1.5% for losses due to snow, and 5% for other sources of error. Identifying and reacting to manageable losses is critical for revenue and O&amp;M efficiency. Monitoring of array performance may be part of contractual agreements between the array owner, the builder, and the utility purchasing the energy produced.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Recently, a method to create "synthetic days" using readily available weather data and verification using the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Solar_Outdoors_Test_Field" title="Open Solar Outdoors Test Field">Open Solar Outdoors Test Field</a> make it possible to predict photovoltaic systems performance with high degrees of accuracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> This method can be used to then determine loss mechanisms on a local scale - such as those from snow<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-open_access_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-open_access-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snow2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-snow2-29">[29]</a></sup> or the effects of surface coatings (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a>) on soiling or snow losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> (Although in heavy snow environments with severe ground interference can result in annual losses from snow of 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>) Access to the Internet has allowed a further improvement in energy monitoring and communication. Dedicated systems are available from a number of vendors. For solar PV systems that use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microinverters" title="Microinverters">microinverters</a> (panel-level DC to AC conversion), module power data is automatically provided. Some systems allow setting performance alerts that trigger phone/email/text warnings when limits are reached. These solutions provide data for the system owner and the installer. Installers are able to remotely monitor multiple installations, and see at-a-glance the status of their entire installed base.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Balance_of_System.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="614" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Balance_of_System.svg/220px-Balance_of_System.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Balance_of_System.svg/330px-Balance_of_System.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Balance_of_System.svg/440px-Balance_of_System.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Balance_of_System.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_system" title="Balance of system">balance of system</a> components of a PV system (BOS) balance the power-generating subsystem of the solar array (left side) with the power-using side of the AC-household devices and the utility grid (right side).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A photovoltaic system for residential, commercial, or industrial energy supply consists of the solar array and a number of components often summarized as the <a href="/wiki/Balance_of_system" title="Balance of system">balance of system</a> (BOS). This term is synonymous with "Balance of Plant" q.v. at <a class="external free" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balance_of_plant">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balance_of_plant</a>. BOS-components include power-conditioning equipment and structures for mounting, typically one or more DC to <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">AC</a> power converters, also known as <a href="/wiki/Power_inverter" title="Power inverter">inverters</a>, an energy storage device, a racking system that supports the solar array, electrical wiring and interconnections, and mounting for other components.</p>
<p>Optionally, a balance of system may include any or all of the following: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_credit" title="Renewable energy credit">renewable energy credit</a> revenue-grade meter, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maximum_power_point_tracker" title="Maximum power point tracker">maximum power point tracker</a> (MPPT), <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">battery</a> system and <a href="/wiki/Battery_charger" title="Battery charger">charger</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPS" title="GPS">GPS</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">solar tracker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy_management_software" title="Energy management software">energy management software</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_irradiance" title="Solar irradiance">solar irradiance</a> sensors, <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometer</a>, or task-specific accessories designed to meet specialized requirements for a system owner. In addition, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics" title="Concentrated photovoltaics">CPV</a> system requires <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_lens" title="Optical lens">optical lenses</a> or mirrors and sometimes a cooling system.</p>
<p>The terms <i>"solar array"</i> and <i>"PV system"</i> are often incorrectly used interchangeably, despite the fact that the solar array does not encompass the entire system. Moreover, <i>"solar panel"</i> is often used as a synonym for <i>"solar module"</i>, although a panel consists of a string of several modules. The term <i>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a>"</i> is also an often used <a href="/wiki/Misnomer" title="Misnomer">misnomer</a> for a PV system.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solar_array">Solar array</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Solar array">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PV_module" title="PV module">PV module</a></div>
<p>Conventional <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">c-Si</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a>, normally wired in series, are encapsulated in a solar module to protect them from the weather. The module consists of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tempered_glass" title="Tempered glass">tempered glass</a> as cover, a soft and flexible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethylene_vinyl_acetate" title="Ethylene vinyl acetate">encapsulant</a>, a rear backsheet made of a weathering and fire-resistant <a href="/wiki/Polyvinyl_fluoride" title="Polyvinyl fluoride">material</a> and an aluminium frame around the outer edge. Electrically connected and mounted on a supporting structure, solar modules build a string of modules, often called solar panel. A solar array consists of one or many such panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> A photovoltaic array, or solar array, is a linked collection of <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>. The power that one module can produce is seldom enough to meet requirements of a home or a business, so the modules are linked together to form an array. Most PV arrays use an <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">inverter</a> to convert the DC power produced by the modules into <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a> that can power <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">lights</a>, motors, and other loads. The modules in a PV array are usually first connected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Series_circuits" title="Series circuits">series</a> to obtain the desired <a href="/wiki/Voltage" title="Voltage">voltage</a>; the individual strings are then connected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parallel_circuits" title="Parallel circuits">parallel</a> to allow the system to produce more <a href="/wiki/Electric_current" title="Electric current">current</a>. Solar panels are typically measured under STC (standard test conditions) or PTC (PVUSA test conditions), in <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Typical panel ratings range from less than 100 watts to over 400 watts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The array rating consists of a summation of the panel ratings, in watts, kilowatts, or megawatts.</p>
<dl>
<dt>Module and efficiency</dt>
</dl>
<p>A typical "150 watt" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PV_module" title="PV module">PV module</a> is about a square meter in size. Such a module may be expected to produce 0.75  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt-hour" title="Kilowatt-hour">kilowatt-hour</a> (kWh) every day, on average, after taking into account the weather and the latitude, for an insolation of 5 sun hours/day. In the last 10 years, the efficiency of average commercial wafer-based <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> modules increased from about 12% to 16% and CdTe module efficiency increased from 9% to 13% during same period. Module output and life degraded by increased temperature. Allowing ambient air to flow over, and if possible behind, PV modules reduces this problem. Effective module lives are typically 25 years or more.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The payback period for an investment in a PV solar installation varies greatly and is typically less useful than a calculation of <a href="/wiki/Return_on_investment" title="Return on investment">return on investment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> While it is typically calculated to be between 10 and 20 years, the financial payback period can be far shorter with incentives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg/300px-Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg/450px-Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg/600px-Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fixed_Tilt_Solar_panel_at_Canterbury_Municipal_Building_Canterbury_New_Hampshire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fixed tilt solar array in of <a href="/wiki/Crystalline_silicon" title="Crystalline silicon">crystalline silicon</a> panels in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury,_New_Hampshire" title="Canterbury, New Hampshire">Canterbury, New Hampshire</a>, United States</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2148" data-file-width="3420" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG/300px-Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG/450px-Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG/600px-Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turisme_de_parcs_fotovoltaics.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Solar array of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_farm" title="Solar farm">solar farm</a> with a few thousand <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_module" title="Solar module">solar modules</a> on the island of <a href="/wiki/Majorca" title="Majorca">Majorca</a>, Spain</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to the low voltage of an individual <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cell</a> (typically ca. 0.5V), several cells are wired <i>(also see <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_renewable_energy#Solar_photovoltaic_power_generation" title="Copper in renewable energy">copper used in PV systems</a>)</i> in series in the manufacture of a "laminate". The laminate is assembled into a protective weatherproof enclosure, thus making a photovoltaic module or <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panel</a>. Modules may then be strung together into a photovoltaic array. In 2012, solar panels available for consumers can have an efficiency of up to about 17%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> while commercially available panels can go as far as 27%. It has been recorded that a group from The Fraunhofer Institute for Solar Energy Systems have created a cell that can reach 44.7% efficiency, which makes scientists' hopes of reaching the 50% efficiency threshold a lot more feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Shading and dirt <span id="Shading_and_dirt"></span></dt>
</dl>
<p>Photovoltaic cell electrical output is extremely sensitive to shading. The effects of this shading are well known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> When even a small portion of a cell, module, or array is shaded, while the remainder is in sunlight, the output falls dramatically due to internal 'short-circuiting' (the electrons reversing course through the shaded portion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/P-n_junction" title="P-n junction">p-n junction</a>). If the current drawn from the series string of cells is no greater than the current that can be produced by the shaded cell, the current (and so power) developed by the string is limited. If enough voltage is available from the rest of the cells in a string, current will be forced through the cell by breaking down the junction in the shaded portion. This breakdown voltage in common cells is between 10 and 30 volts. Instead of adding to the power produced by the panel, the shaded cell absorbs power, turning it into heat. Since the reverse voltage of a shaded cell is much greater than the forward voltage of an illuminated cell, one shaded cell can absorb the power of many other cells in the string, disproportionately affecting panel output. For example, a shaded cell may drop 8 volts, instead of adding 0.5 volts, at a particular current level, thereby absorbing the power produced by 16 other cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UE03_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-UE03-46">[46]</a></sup> It is, thus important that a PV installation is not shaded by trees or other obstructions.</p>
<p>Several methods have been developed to determine shading losses from trees to PV systems over both large regions using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LiDAR" title="LiDAR">LiDAR</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> but also at an individual system level using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sketchup" title="Sketchup">sketchup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Most modules have bypass diodes between each cell or string of cells that minimize the effects of shading and only lose the power of the shaded portion of the array. The main job of the bypass diode is to eliminate hot spots that form on cells that can cause further damage to the array, and cause fires. Sunlight can be absorbed by dust, snow, or other impurities at the surface of the module. This can reduce the light that strikes the cells. In general these losses aggregated over the year are small even for locations in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-open_access_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-open_access-28">[28]</a></sup> Maintaining a clean module surface will increase output performance over the life of the module. Google found that cleaning the flat mounted solar panels after 15 months increased their output by almost 100%, but that the 5% tilted arrays were adequately cleaned by rainwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snow2_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-snow2-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Insolation and energy</dt>
</dl>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="1500" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png/220px-SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png/330px-SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png/440px-SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SolarGIS-Solar-map-World-map-en.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">solar potential</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_insolation" title="Solar insolation">Solar insolation</a> is made up of direct, diffuse, and reflected <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">radiation</a>. The absorption factor of a PV cell is deﬁned as the fraction of incident solar irradiance that is absorbed by the cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> At high noon on a cloudless day at the equator, the power of the sun is about 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">kW</a>/m²,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> on the Earth's surface, to a plane that is perpendicular to the sun's rays. As such, PV arrays can <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">track the sun</a> through each day to greatly enhance energy collection. However, tracking devices add cost, and require maintenance, so it is more common for PV arrays to have fixed mounts that tilt the array and face <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_noon" title="Solar noon">solar noon</a> (approximately due south in the Northern Hemisphere or due north in the Southern Hemisphere). The tilt angle, from horizontal, can be varied for season,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> but if fixed, should be set to give optimal array output during the peak electrical demand portion of a typical year for a stand-alone system. This optimal module tilt angle is not necessarily identical to the tilt angle for maximum annual array energy output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The optimization of the photovoltaic system for a specific environment can be complicated as issues of solar flux, soiling, and snow losses should be taken into effect. In addition, recent work has shown that spectral effects can play a role in optimal photovoltaic material selection. For example, the spectral <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> can play a significant role in output depending on the surface around the photovoltaic system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> and the type of solar cell material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> For the weather and latitudes of the United States and Europe, typical insolation ranges from 4 kWh/m²/day in northern climes to 6.5 kWh/m²/day in the sunniest regions. A photovoltaic installation in the southern latitudes of Europe or the United States may expect to produce 1 kWh/m²/day. A typical 1 kW photovoltaic installation in Australia or the southern latitudes of Europe or United States, may produce 3.5–5 kWh per day, dependent on location, orientation, tilt, insolation and other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert, with less cloud cover and a better solar angle, one could ideally obtain closer to 8.3 kWh/m²/day provided the nearly ever present wind would not blow sand onto the units. The area of the Sahara desert is over 9 million km². 90,600 km², or about 1%, could generate as much electricity as all of the world's power plants combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mounting">Mounting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mounting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_mounting_system" title="Photovoltaic mounting system">Photovoltaic mounting system</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG/220px-Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG/330px-Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG/440px-Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pellworm_Solarkrftanlage_MS_P4140082.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 23-year-old, ground mounted PV system from the 1980s on a <a href="/wiki/North_Frisian_Islands" title="North Frisian Islands">North Frisian Island</a>, Germany. The modules conversion efficiency was only 12%.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Modules are assembled into arrays on some kind of mounting system, which may be classified as ground mount, roof mount or pole mount. For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_park" title="Solar park">solar parks</a> a large rack is mounted on the ground, and the modules mounted on the rack. For buildings, many different racks have been devised for pitched roofs. For flat roofs, racks, bins and building integrated solutions are used.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Solar panel racks mounted on top of poles can be stationary or moving, see Trackers below. Side-of-pole mounts are suitable for situations where a pole has something else mounted at its top, such as a light fixture or an antenna. Pole mounting raises what would otherwise be a ground mounted array above weed shadows and livestock, and may satisfy electrical code requirements regarding inaccessibility of exposed wiring. Pole mounted panels are open to more cooling air on their underside, which increases performance. A multiplicity of pole top racks can be formed into a parking carport or other shade structure. A rack which does not follow the sun from left to right may allow seasonal adjustment up or down.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabling">Cabling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cabling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Solar_cable" title="Solar cable">Solar cable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">Electrical wiring</a></div>
<p>Due to their outdoor usage, <a href="/wiki/Solar_cable" title="Solar cable">solar cables</a> are specifically designed to be resistant against <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV" title="UV">UV</a> radiation and extremely high temperature fluctuations and are generally unaffected by the weather. A number of standards specify the usage of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">electrical wiring</a> in PV systems, such as the <a href="/wiki/IEC_60364" title="IEC 60364">IEC 60364</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a>, in section 712 <i>"Solar photovoltaic (PV) power supply systems"</i>, the British Standard <a href="/wiki/BS_7671" title="BS 7671">BS 7671</a>, incorporating regulations relating to <a href="/wiki/Microgeneration" title="Microgeneration">microgeneration</a> and photovoltaic systems, and the US <a href="/wiki/Solar_cable#UL4703_standard" title="Solar cable">UL4703 standard</a>, in subject 4703 <i>"Photovoltaic Wire"</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tracker">Tracker</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tracker">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">Solar tracker</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg/200px-ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg/300px-ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg/400px-ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ZomeworksTrackerHead5816.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1998 model of a passive solar tracker, viewed from underneath.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Solar_tracker" title="Solar tracker">solar tracking system</a> tilts a solar panel throughout the day. Depending on the type of tracking system, the panel is either aimed directly at the sun or the brightest area of a partly clouded sky. Trackers greatly enhance early morning and late afternoon performance, increasing the total amount of power produced by a system by about 20–25% for a single axis tracker and about 30% or more for a dual axis tracker, depending on latitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Trackers are effective in regions that receive a large portion of sunlight directly. In diffuse light (i.e. under cloud or fog), tracking has little or no value. Because most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics" title="Concentrated photovoltaics">concentrated photovoltaics</a> systems are very sensitive to the sunlight's angle, tracking systems allow them to produce useful power for more than a brief period each day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Tracking systems improve performance for two main reasons. First, when a solar panel is perpendicular to the sunlight, it receives more light on its surface than if it were angled. Second, direct light is used more efficiently than angled light.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Special <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-reflective" title="Anti-reflective">Anti-reflective coatings</a> can improve solar panel efficiency for direct and angled light, somewhat reducing the benefit of tracking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trackers and sensors to optimise the performance are often seen as optional, but tracking systems can increase viable output by up to 45%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> PV arrays that approach or exceed one megawatt often use solar trackers. Accounting for clouds, and the fact that most of the world is not on the equator, and that the sun sets in the evening, the correct measure of solar power is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> – the average number of kilowatt-hours per square meter per day. For the weather and latitudes of the United States and Europe, typical insolation ranges from 2.26 kWh/m²/day in northern climes to 5.61 kWh/m²/day in the sunniest regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>For large systems, the energy gained by using tracking systems can outweigh the added complexity (trackers can increase efficiency by 30% or more). For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_large_scale_solar_power_plant" title="Very large scale solar power plant">very large systems</a>, the added maintenance of tracking is a substantial detriment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Tracking is not required for flat panel and low-concentration <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics" title="Concentrated photovoltaics">photovoltaic systems</a>. For high-concentration photovoltaic systems, dual axis tracking is a necessity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Pricing trends affect the balance between adding more stationary solar panels versus having fewer panels that track. When solar panel prices drop, trackers become a less attractive option.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inverter">Inverter</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Inverter">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">Solar inverter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_micro-inverter" title="Solar micro-inverter">Solar micro-inverter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Power_inverter" title="Power inverter">Power inverter</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Central_inverter_cutout.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1002" data-file-width="746" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Central_inverter_cutout.jpg/220px-Central_inverter_cutout.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Central_inverter_cutout.jpg/330px-Central_inverter_cutout.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Central_inverter_cutout.jpg/440px-Central_inverter_cutout.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Central_inverter_cutout.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Central inverter with AC and DC disconnects (on the side), monitoring gateway, transformer isolation and interactive LCD.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG/220px-Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG/330px-Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG/440px-Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vermont_Law_School_Solar_Panel_Array-4.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
String inverter (left), generation meter, and AC disconnect (right). A modern 2013 installation in <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, United States.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Systems designed to deliver <a href="/wiki/Alternating_current" title="Alternating current">alternating current</a> (AC), such as <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_system#Grid-connected_applications" title="Photovoltaic system">grid-connected applications</a> need an inverter to convert the <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> (DC) from the solar modules to AC. Grid connected inverters must supply AC electricity in sinusoidal form, synchronized to the grid frequency, limit feed in voltage to no higher than the grid voltage and disconnect from the grid if the grid voltage is turned off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Islanding inverters need only produce regulated voltages and frequencies in a sinusoidal waveshape as no synchronisation or co-ordination with grid supplies is required.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Solar_inverter" title="Solar inverter">solar inverter</a> may connect to a string of solar panels. In some installations a <a href="/wiki/Solar_micro-inverter" title="Solar micro-inverter">solar micro-inverter</a> is connected at each solar panel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> For safety reasons a circuit breaker is provided both on the AC and DC side to enable maintenance. AC output may be connected through an <a href="/wiki/Electricity_meter" title="Electricity meter">electricity meter</a> into the public grid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Module" title="Module">modules</a> in the system determines the total DC watts capable of being generated by the solar array; however, the inverter ultimately governs the amount of AC watts that can be distributed for consumption. For example, a PV system comprising 11 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilowatt" title="Kilowatt">kilowatts</a> DC (kW<sub>DC</sub>) worth of PV modules, paired with one 10-kilowatt AC (kW<sub>AC</sub>) inverter, will be limited to the inverter's output of 10 kW. As of 2014, conversion efficiency for state-of-the-art converters reached more than 98 percent. While string inverters are used in residential to medium-sized commercial PV systems, central inverters cover the large commercial and utility-scale market. Market-share for central and string inverters are about 50 percent and 48 percent, respectively, leaving less than 2 percent to micro-inverters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fraunhofer-report-september-2014_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-fraunhofer-report-september-2014-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maximum_power_point_tracking" title="Maximum power point tracking">Maximum power point tracking</a> (MPPT) is a technique that grid connected inverters use to get the maximum possible power from the photovoltaic array. In order to do so, the inverter's MPPT system digitally samples the solar array's ever changing power output and applies the proper resistance to find the optimal <i>maximum power point</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-islanding" title="Anti-islanding">Anti-islanding</a> is a protection mechanism that immediately shuts down the inverter preventing it from generating AC power when the connection to the load no longer exists. This happens, for example, in the case of a blackout. Without this protection, the supply line would become an "island" with power surrounded by a "sea" of unpowered lines, as the solar array continues to deliver DC power during the power outage. Islanding is a hazard to utility workers, who may not realize that an AC circuit is still powered, and it may prevent automatic re-connection of devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
