<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery">Battery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Battery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">Rechargeable battery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">Battery (electricity)</a></div>
<p>Although still expensive, PV systems increasingly use rechargeable batteries to store a surplus to be later used at night. <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage#Batteries" title="Grid energy storage">Batteries used for grid-storage</a> also stabilize the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a> by <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage#Load_leveling" title="Grid energy storage">leveling out peak loads</a>, and play an important role in a <a href="/wiki/Smart_grid" title="Smart grid">smart grid</a>, as they can charge during periods of low demand and feed their stored energy into the grid when demand is high.</p>
<p>Common battery technologies used in today's PV systems include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valve_regulated_lead-acid_battery" title="Valve regulated lead-acid battery">valve regulated lead-acid battery</a>– a modified version of the conventional <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead–acid battery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93cadmium_battery" title="Nickel–cadmium battery">nickel–cadmium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion</a> batteries. Compared to the other types, lead-acid batteries have a shorter lifetime and lower energy density. However, due to their high reliability, low self discharge as well as low investment and maintenance costs, they are currently the predominant technology used in small-scale, residential PV systems, as lithium-ion batteries are still being developed and about 3.5 times as expensive as lead-acid batteries. Furthermore, as storage devices for PV systems are stationary, the lower energy and power density and therefore higher weight of lead-acid batteries are not as critical as, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric transportation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethz-harvard_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ethz-harvard-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4,9</sup> Other rechargeable batteries that are considered for distributed PV systems include <a href="/wiki/Sodium%E2%80%93sulfur_battery" title="Sodium–sulfur battery">sodium–sulfur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vanadium_redox_battery" title="Vanadium redox battery">vanadium redox</a> batteries, two prominent types of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molten_salt_battery" title="Molten salt battery">molten salt</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Flow_battery" title="Flow battery">flow</a> battery, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethz-harvard_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-ethz-harvard-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:4</sup> In 2015, Tesla motors launched the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Powerwall_(Tesla)" title="Powerwall (Tesla)">Powerwall</a>, a rechargeable lithium-ion battery with the aim to revolutionize energy consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>PV systems with an integrated battery solution also need a <a href="/wiki/Charge_controller" title="Charge controller">charge controller</a>, as the varying voltage and current from the solar array requires constant adjustment to prevent damage from overcharging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Basic charge controllers may simply turn the PV panels on and off, or may meter out pulses of energy as needed, a strategy called PWM or <a href="/wiki/Pulse-width_modulation" title="Pulse-width modulation">pulse-width modulation</a>. More advanced charge controllers will incorporate <a href="/wiki/Maximum_power_point_tracking" title="Maximum power point tracking">MPPT</a> logic into their battery charging algorithms. Charge controllers may also divert energy to some purpose other than battery charging. Rather than simply shut off the free PV energy when not needed, a user may choose to heat air or water once the battery is full.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monitoring_and_metering">Monitoring and metering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Monitoring and metering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">Net metering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_metering" title="Energy metering">Energy metering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smart_meter" title="Smart meter">Smart meter</a></div>
<p>The metering must be able to accumulate energy units in both directions or two meters must be used. Many meters accumulate bidirectionally, some systems use two meters, but a unidirectional meter (with detent) will not accumulate energy from any resultant feed into the grid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> In some countries, for installations over 30 <a href="/wiki/Nominal_power_(photovoltaic)#Watt-peak" title="Nominal power (photovoltaic)">kW<sub>p</sub></a> a frequency and a voltage monitor with disconnection of all phases is required. This is done where more solar power is being generated than can be accommodated by the utility, and the excess can not either be exported or <a href="/wiki/Grid_energy_storage" title="Grid energy storage">stored</a>. Grid operators historically have needed to provide transmission lines and generation capacity. Now they need to also provide storage. This is normally hydro-storage, but other means of storage are used. Initially storage was used so that baseload generators could operate at full output. With <a href="/wiki/Variable_renewable_energy" title="Variable renewable energy">variable renewable energy</a>, storage is needed to allow power generation whenever it is available, and consumption whenever it is needed.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG/220px-Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG/330px-Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG/440px-Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stromz%C3%A4hler_in_Kanada.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Canadian electricity meter</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The two variables a grid operator have are storing electricity for <i>when</i> it is needed, or transmitting it to <i>where</i> it is needed. If both of those fail, installations over 30kWp can automatically shut down, although in practice all inverters maintain voltage regulation and stop supplying power if the load is inadequate. Grid operators have the option of curtailing excess generation from large systems, although this is more commonly done with wind power than solar power, and results in a substantial loss of revenue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Three-phase inverters have the unique option of supplying reactive power which can be advantageous in matching load requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Photovoltaic systems need to be monitored to detect breakdown and optimize their operation. There are several <i>photovoltaic monitoring</i> strategies depending on the output of the installation and its nature. Monitoring can be performed on site or remotely. It can measure production only, retrieve all the data from the inverter or retrieve all of the data from the communicating equipment (probes, meters, etc.). Monitoring tools can be dedicated to supervision only or offer additional functions. Individual inverters and battery charge controllers may include monitoring using manufacturer specific protocols and software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Energy metering of an inverter may be of limited accuracy and not suitable for revenue metering purposes. A third-party data acquisition system can monitor multiple inverters, using the inverter manufacturer's protocols, and also acquire weather-related information. Independent <a href="/wiki/Smart_meter" title="Smart meter">smart meters</a> may measure the total energy production of a PV array system. Separate measures such as satellite image analysis or a solar radiation meter (a <a href="/wiki/Pyranometer" title="Pyranometer">pyranometer</a>) can be used to estimate total insolation for comparison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Data collected from a monitoring system can be <a href="/wiki/List_of_monitored_photovoltaic_power_stations" title="List of monitored photovoltaic power stations">displayed remotely</a> over the World Wide Web, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OSOTF" title="OSOTF">OSOTF</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_systems">Other systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This section includes systems that are either highly specialized and uncommon or still an emerging new technology with limited significance. However, <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">standalone</a> or off-grid systems take a special place. They were the most common type of systems during the 1980s and 1990s, when PV technology was still very expensive and a pure niche market of small scale applications. Only in places where no electrical grid was available, they were economically viable. Although new stand-alone systems are still being deployed all around the world, their contribution to the overall installed photovoltaic capacity is decreasing. In Europe, off-grid systems account for 1 percent of installed capacity. In the United States, they account for about 10 percent. Off-grid systems are still common in Australia and South Korea, and in many developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epia-outlook-2014_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-epia-outlook-2014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:14</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="CPV">CPV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: CPV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaics" title="Concentrator photovoltaics">Concentrator photovoltaics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2120" data-file-width="2816" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg/220px-Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg/330px-Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg/440px-Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Concentraci%C3%B3_Fotovoltaica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrator_photovoltaic" title="Concentrator photovoltaic">Concentrator photovoltaic</a> (CPV) in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Concentrator photovoltaics (CPV) and <i>high concentrator photovoltaic</i> (HCPV) systems use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_lens" title="Optical lens">optical lenses</a> or curved mirrors to concentrate sunlight onto small but highly efficient solar cells. Besides concentrating optics, CPV systems sometime use solar trackers and cooling systems and are more expensive.</p>
<p>Especially HCPV systems are best suited in location with high solar irradiance, concentrating sunlight up to 400 times or more, with efficiencies of 24–28 percent, exceeding those of regular systems. Various designs of CPV and HCPV systems are commercially available but not very common. However, ongoing research and development is taking place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fraunhofer-PR-2014_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Fraunhofer-PR-2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:26</sup></p>
<p>CPV is often confused with CSP (<a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a>) that does not use photovoltaics. Both technologies favor locations that receive much sunlight and are directly competing with each other.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid">Hybrid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Hybrid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hybrid_Power_System.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="493" data-file-width="557" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hybrid_Power_System.gif/220px-Hybrid_Power_System.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hybrid_Power_System.gif/330px-Hybrid_Power_System.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Hybrid_Power_System.gif/440px-Hybrid_Power_System.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hybrid_Power_System.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Solar_hybrid_power_systems" title="Solar hybrid power systems">wind-solar PV hybrid system</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A hybrid system combines PV with other forms of generation, usually a diesel generator. Biogas is also used. The other form of generation may be a type able to modulate power output as a function of demand. However more than one renewable form of energy may be used e.g. wind. The photovoltaic power generation serves to reduce the consumption of non renewable fuel. Hybrid systems are most often found on islands. <a href="/wiki/Pellworm" title="Pellworm">Pellworm</a> island in Germany and <a href="/wiki/Kythnos" title="Kythnos">Kythnos</a> island in Greece are notable examples (both are combined with wind).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The Kythnos plant has reduced diesel consumption by 11.2%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, a case-study conducted in seven countries concluded that in all cases generating costs can be reduced by hybridising mini-grids and isolated grids. However, financing costs for such hybrids are crucial and largely depend on the ownership structure of the power plant. While cost reductions for state-owned utilities can be significant, the study also identified economic benefits to be insignificant or even negative for non-public utilities, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_power_producer" title="Independent power producer">independent power producers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>There has also been recent work showing that the PV penetration limit can be increased by deploying a distributed network of PV+CHP hybrid systems in the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The temporal distribution of solar flux, electrical and heating requirements for representative U.S. single family residences were analyzed and the results clearly show that hybridizing CHP with PV can enable additional PV deployment above what is possible with a conventional centralized electric generation system. This theory was reconfirmed with numerical simulations using per second solar flux data to determine that the necessary battery backup to provide for such a hybrid system is possible with relatively small and inexpensive battery systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In addition, large PV+CHP systems are possible for institutional buildings, which again provide back up for intermittent PV and reduce CHP runtime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_thermal_hybrid_solar_collector" title="Photovoltaic thermal hybrid solar collector">PVT system</a></b> (hybrid PV/T), also known as <i>photovoltaic thermal hybrid solar collectors</i> convert solar radiation into thermal and electrical energy. Such a system combines a solar (PV) module with a <a href="/wiki/Solar_thermal_collector" title="Solar thermal collector">solar thermal collector</a> in a complementary way.</li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaics_and_thermal" title="Concentrated photovoltaics and thermal">CPVT system</a></b>. A <i>concentrated photovoltaic thermal hybrid</i> (CPVT) system is similar to a PVT system. It uses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentrated_photovoltaic" title="Concentrated photovoltaic">concentrated photovoltaics</a> (CPV) instead of conventional PV technology, and combines it with a solar thermal collector.</li>
<li><b>CPV/CSP system</b>. A novel solar CPV/CSP hybrid system has been proposed recently, combining concentrator photovoltaics with the non-PV technology of <a href="/wiki/Concentrated_solar_power" title="Concentrated solar power">concentrated solar power</a> (CSP), or also known as concentrated solar thermal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PV_diesel_system" title="PV diesel system">PV diesel system</a></b>. It combines a photovoltaic system with a <a href="/wiki/Diesel_generator" title="Diesel generator">diesel generator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Combinations with <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_renewable_energy_system" title="Hybrid renewable energy system">other renewables</a> are possible and include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_turbines" title="Wind turbines">wind turbines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floating_solar_arrays">Floating solar arrays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Floating solar arrays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Floating_solar" title="Floating solar">Floating solar</a> arrays are PV systems that float on the surface of drinking water reservoirs, quarry lakes, irrigation canals or remediation and tailing ponds. These systems are called "floatovoltaics" when used only for electrical production or "aquavoltaics" when such systems are used to synergistically enhance <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> A small number of such systems exist in France, India, Japan, South Korea, the United Kingdom, Singapore and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The systems are said to have advantages over photovoltaics on land. The cost of land is more expensive, and there are fewer rules and regulations for structures built on bodies of water not used for recreation. Unlike most land-based solar plants, floating arrays can be unobtrusive because they are hidden from public view. They achieve higher efficiencies than PV panels on land, because water cools the panels. The panels have a special coating to prevent rust or corrosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2008, the Far Niente Winery in Oakville, California, pioneered the world's first floatovoltaic system by installing 994 solar PV modules with a total capacity of 477 kW onto 130 pontoons and floating them on the winery's irrigation pond.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The primary benefit of such a system is that it avoids the need to sacrifice valuable land area that could be used for another purpose. In the case of the Far Niente Winery, it saved three-quarters of an acre that would have been required for a land-based system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Another benefit of a floatovoltaic system is that the panels are kept at a cooler temperature than they would be on land, leading to a higher efficiency of solar energy conversion. The floating PV array also reduces the amount of water lost through evaporation and inhibits the growth of algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Utility-scale floating PV farms are starting to be built. The multinational electronics and ceramics manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Kyocera" title="Kyocera">Kyocera</a> will develop the world's largest, a 13.4 MW farm on the reservoir above Yamakura Dam in <a href="/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture" title="Chiba Prefecture">Chiba Prefecture</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> using 50,000 solar panels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Salt-water resistant floating farms are also being considered for ocean use, with experiments in Thailand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The largest so far announced floatovoltaic project is a 350 MW power station in the Amazon region of Brazil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_current_grid">Direct current grid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Direct current grid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>DC grids are found in electric powered transport: railways trams and trolleybuses. A few pilot plants for such applications have been built, such as the tram depots in Hannover Leinhausen, using photovoltaic contributors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> and Geneva (Bachet de Pesay).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The 150 kW<sub>p</sub> Geneva site feeds 600V DC directly into the tram/trolleybus electricity network whereas before it provided about 15% of the electricity at its opening in 1999.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standalone"><span id="Standalone_system"></span> Standalone</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Standalone">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:368px;max-width:368px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG/230px-P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG/345px-P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG/460px-P1020564_-_Refugi_d%27Estany_Llong.JPG 2x" width="230"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An isolated mountain hut in <a href="/wiki/Catalonia" title="Catalonia">Catalonia</a>, Spain</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TicketParkingMeter.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="960" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/TicketParkingMeter.jpg/130px-TicketParkingMeter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/TicketParkingMeter.jpg/195px-TicketParkingMeter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/TicketParkingMeter.jpg/260px-TicketParkingMeter.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Solar parking meter in <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Stand-alone_power_system" title="Stand-alone power system">stand-alone</a> or off-grid system is not connected to the <a href="/wiki/Electrical_grid" title="Electrical grid">electrical grid</a>. Standalone systems vary widely in size and application from <a href="/wiki/Solar-powered_watch" title="Solar-powered watch">wristwatches</a> or <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_silicon#Solar_cells" title="Amorphous silicon">calculators</a> to remote buildings or <a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">spacecraft</a>. If the load is to be supplied independently of solar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a>, the generated power is stored and buffered with a battery. In non-portable applications where weight is not an issue, such as in buildings, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lead_acid_battery" title="Lead acid battery">lead acid batteries</a> are most commonly used for their low cost and tolerance for abuse.</p>
<p>A charge controller may be incorporated in the system to avoid battery damage by excessive charging or discharging. It may also help to optimize production from the solar array using a maximum power point tracking technique (<a href="/wiki/Maximum_power_point_tracking" title="Maximum power point tracking">MPPT</a>). However, in simple PV systems where the PV module voltage is matched to the battery voltage, the use of MPPT electronics is generally considered unnecessary, since the battery voltage is stable enough to provide near-maximum power collection from the PV module. In small devices (e.g. calculators, parking meters) only <a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">direct current</a> (DC) is consumed. In larger systems (e.g. buildings, remote water pumps) AC is usually required. To convert the DC from the modules or batteries into AC, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverter_(electrical)" title="Inverter (electrical)">inverter</a> is used.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a> settings, the array may be used to directly power DC <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumps</a>, without the need for an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverter_(electrical)" title="Inverter (electrical)">inverter</a>. In remote settings such as mountainous areas, islands, or other places where a power grid is unavailable, solar arrays can be used as the sole source of electricity, usually by charging a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Storage_battery" title="Storage battery">storage battery</a>. Stand-alone systems closely relate to <a href="/wiki/Microgeneration" title="Microgeneration">microgeneration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distributed_generation" title="Distributed generation">distributed generation</a>.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Pico PV systems</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>The smallest, often portable photovoltaic systems are called pico solar PV systems, or pico solar. They mostly combine a rechargeable battery and charge controller, with a very small PV panel. The panel's nominal capacity is just a few watt-peak (1–10 W<sub>p</sub>) and its area less than a tenth of a square meter, or one square foot, in size. A large range of different applications can be solar powered such as music players, fans, portable lamps, security lights, solar lighting kits, solar lanterns and street light <i>(see below)</i>, phone chargers, radios, or even small, seven-inch LCD televisions, that run on less than ten watts. As it is the case for power generation from <a href="/wiki/Pico_hydro" title="Pico hydro">pico hydro</a>, pico PV systems are useful in small, rural communities that require only a small amount of electricity. Since the efficiency of many appliances have improved considerably, in particular due to the usage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LED_lights" title="LED lights">LED lights</a> and efficient rechargeable batteries, pico solar has become an affordable alternative, especially in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> The metric prefix <i><a href="/wiki/Pico-" title="Pico-">pico-</a></i> stands for a <i>trillionth</i> to indicate the smallness of the system's electric power.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Solar street lights</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Solar_street_light" title="Solar street light">Solar street lights</a> raised light sources which are powered by photovoltaic panels generally mounted on the lighting structure. The solar array of such off-grid PV system charges a <a href="/wiki/Rechargeable_battery" title="Rechargeable battery">rechargeable battery</a>, which powers a fluorescent or LED lamp during the night. Solar street lights are stand-alone power systems, and have the advantage of savings on trenching, landscaping, and maintenance costs, as well as on the electric bills, despite their higher initial cost compared to conventional street lighting. They are designed with sufficiently large batteries to ensure operation for at least a week and even in the worst situation, they are expected to dim only slightly.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Telecommunication and signaling</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>Solar PV power is ideally suited for telecommunication applications such as local telephone exchange, radio and TV broadcasting, microwave and other forms of electronic communication links. This is because, in most telecommunication application, storage batteries are already in use and the electrical system is basically DC. In hilly and mountainous terrain, radio and TV signals may not reach as they get blocked or reflected back due to undulating terrain. At these locations, <a href="/wiki/Low-power_broadcasting" title="Low-power broadcasting">low power transmitters</a> are installed to receive and retransmit the signal for local population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Solar vehicles</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Solar_vehicle" title="Solar vehicle">Solar vehicle</a>, whether ground, water, air or space vehicles may obtain some or all of the energy required for their operation from the sun. Surface vehicles generally require higher power levels than can be sustained by a practically sized solar array, so a battery assists in meeting peak power demand, and the solar array recharges it. Space vehicles have successfully used solar photovoltaic systems for years of operation, eliminating the weight of fuel or primary batteries.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Solar pumps</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>One of the most cost effective solar applications is a solar powered pump, as it is far cheaper to purchase a solar panel than it is to run power lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> They often meet a need for water beyond the reach of power lines, taking the place of a <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmill</a> or <a href="/wiki/Windpump" title="Windpump">windpump</a>. One common application is the filling of livestock watering tanks, so that grazing cattle may drink. Another is the refilling of drinking water storage tanks on remote or self-sufficient homes.</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Spacecraft</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Solar_panels_on_spacecraft" title="Solar panels on spacecraft">Solar panels on spacecraft</a> have been one of the first applications of photovoltaics since the launch of <a href="/wiki/Vanguard_1" title="Vanguard 1">Vanguard 1</a> in 1958, the first satellite to use solar cells. Contrary to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sputnik" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a>, the first artificial satellite to orbit the planet, that ran out of batteries within 21 days due to the lack of solar-power, most modern <a href="/wiki/Communications_satellite" title="Communications satellite">communications satellites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_solar_system" title="Inner solar system">inner solar system</a> rely on the use of solar panels to derive electricity from sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><b>Do it yourself community</b></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>With a growing interest in environmentally friendly green energy, an increasing number of hobbyists in the <a href="/wiki/Do_it_yourself" title="Do it yourself">DIY</a>-community have endeavored to build their own solar PV systems from kits<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> or partly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DIY" title="DIY">DIY</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Usually, the DIY-community uses inexpensive<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> or high efficiency systems<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> (such as those with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_tracking" title="Solar tracking">solar tracking</a>) to generate their own power. As a result, the DIY-systems often end up cheaper than their commercial counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Often, the system is also hooked up into the regular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_grid" title="Power grid">power grid</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Net_metering" title="Net metering">net metering</a> instead of a battery for backup. These systems usually generate power amount of ~2 kW or less. Through the internet, the community is now able to obtain plans to (partly) construct the system and there is a growing trend toward building them for domestic requirements.</dd>
</dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Costs_and_economy">Costs and economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Costs and economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner noresize" style="width:267px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="width:265px;">
<div style="padding-top: 10px; text-align: center;"><b>Median installed system prices for residential PV Systems in Japan, Germany and the United States ($/W)</b></div>
<div style="font-size: 80%;">

<div class="thumbcaption">History of solar rooftop prices since 2006. Comparison in US$ per installed watt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NREL-deployment2014_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-NREL-deployment2014-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-german-price-index_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-german-price-index-125">[125]</a></sup>
<div style="padding-left: 6px; margin: 4px 0; width: 100%; font-size: 0.9em; text-align: left; font-weight: bold;">
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #FF0000; background-color: #FF4D4D; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Japan" title="Solar power in Japan">Japan</a></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #0066CC; background-color: #0080FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_the_United_States" title="Solar power in the United States">United States</a></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #009900; background-color: #00CC00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Solar_power_in_Germany" title="Solar power in Germany">Germany</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The cost of producing photovotaic cells have dropped due to economies of scale in production and technological advances in manufacturing. For large-scale installations, prices below $1.00 per watt were common by 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> A price decrease of 50% had been achieved in Europe from 2006 to 2011 and there is a potential to lower the generation cost by 50% by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Crystal silicon <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">solar cells</a> have largely been replaced by less expensive multicrystalline silicon solar cells, and thin film silicon solar cells have also been developed recently at lower costs of production. Although they are reduced in energy conversion efficiency from single crystalline "siwafers", they are also much easier to produce at comparably lower costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>The table below shows the total cost in US cents per kWh of electricity generated by a photovoltaic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The row headings on the left show the total cost, per peak kilowatt (kW<sub>p</sub>), of a photovoltaic installation. Photovoltaic system costs have been declining and in Germany, for example, were reported to have fallen to USD 1389/kW<sub>p</sub> by the end of 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pvgde:priceindex_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-pvgde:priceindex-131">[131]</a></sup> The column headings across the top refer to the annual energy output in kWh expected from each installed kW<sub>p</sub>. This varies by geographic region because the average <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a> depends on the average cloudiness and the thickness of atmosphere traversed by the sunlight. It also depends on the path of the sun relative to the panel and the horizon. Panels are usually mounted at an angle based on latitude, and often they are adjusted seasonally to meet the changing solar <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_tracking" title="Solar tracking">Solar tracking</a> can also be utilized to access even more perpendicular sunlight, thereby raising the total energy output.</p>
<p>The calculated values in the table reflect the total cost in cents per kWh produced. They assume a 10% total capital cost (for instance 4% <a href="/wiki/Interest_rate" title="Interest rate">interest rate</a>, 1% operating and maintenance cost,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straight-line_depreciation" title="Straight-line depreciation">depreciation</a> of the capital outlay over 20 years). Normally, photovoltaic modules have a 25-year warranty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="System_cost_2013">System cost 2013</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: System cost 2013">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In its 2014 edition of the "Technology Roadmap: Solar Photovoltaic Energy" report, the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a> (IEA) published prices in US$ per watt for residential, commercial and utility-scale PV systems for eight major markets in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEA-roadmap-PV-2014-7">[7]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standardization">Standardization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Standardization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Increasing use of photovoltaic systems and integration of photovoltaic power into existing structures and techniques of supply and distribution increases the value of general standards and definitions for photovoltaic components and systems.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The standards are compiled at the <a href="/wiki/International_Electrotechnical_Commission" title="International Electrotechnical Commission">International Electrotechnical Commission</a> (IEC) and apply to efficiency, durability and safety of cells, modules, simulation programs, plug connectors and cables, mounting systems, overall efficiency of inverters etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planning_and_permit">Planning and permit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Planning and permit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While article 690 of the National Electric Code provides general guidelines for the installation of photovoltaic systems, these guidelines may be superseded by local laws and regulations. Often a permit is required necessitating plan submissions and structural calculations before work may begin. Additionally, many locales require the work to be performed under the guidance of a licensed electrician. Check with the local City/County AHJ (Authority Having Jurisdiction) to ensure compliance with any applicable laws or regulations.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authority_Having_Jurisdiction" title="Authority Having Jurisdiction">Authority Having Jurisdiction</a> (AHJ) will review designs and issue permits, before construction can lawfully begin. Electrical installation practices must comply with standards set forth within the <a href="/wiki/National_Electrical_Code" title="National Electrical Code">National Electrical Code</a> (NEC) and be inspected by the AHJ to ensure compliance with <a href="/wiki/Building_code" title="Building code">building code</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_code" title="Electrical code">electrical code</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_safety" title="Fire safety">fire safety</a> code. Jurisdictions may require that equipment has been tested, certified, listed, and labeled by at least one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationally_Recognized_Testing_Laboratories" title="Nationally Recognized Testing Laboratories">Nationally Recognized Testing Laboratories</a> (NRTL). Despite the complicated installation process, a recent list of solar contractors shows a majority of installation companies were founded since 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_Power_World_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solar_Power_World-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_regulations">National regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: National regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl>
<dt>United Kingdom</dt>
</dl>
<p>In the UK, PV installations are generally considered permitted development and don't require planning permission. If the property is listed or in a designated area (National Park, Area of Outstanding Natural Beauty, Site of Special Scientific Interest or Norfolk Broads) then planning permission is required.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>United States</dt>
</dl>
<p>In the US, many localities require a permit to install a photovoltaic system. A grid-tied system normally requires a licensed electrician to make the connection between the system and the grid-connected wiring of the building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Installers who meet these qualifications are located in almost every state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_Power_World_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Solar_Power_World-137">[137]</a></sup> The State of California prohibits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeowners%27_association" title="Homeowners' association">homeowners' associations</a> from restricting solar devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Spain</dt>
</dl>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_Spain" title="Renewable energy in Spain">Spain generates around 40%</a> of its electricity via photovoltaic and other renewable energy sources, and cities such as Huelva and Seville boast nearly 3,000 hours of sunshine per year, Spain has issued a solar tax to account for the debt created by the investment done by the Spanish government. Those who do not connect to the grid can face up to a fine of 30 million euros ($40 million USD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Photovoltaic_system&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
